<li>requiring an authentication token in the <a href="/wiki/POST_(HTTP)" title="POST (HTTP)">POST</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GET_(HTTP)" title="GET (HTTP)">GET</a> parameters for any response that returns private information.</li>
</ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Misplaced_trust_in_the_client">Misplaced trust in the client</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=JavaScript&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Misplaced trust in the client">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Developers of client-server applications must recognize that untrusted clients may be under the control of attackers. The application author cannot assume that his JavaScript code will run as intended (or at all) because any secret embedded in the code could be extracted by a determined adversary. Some implications are:</p>
<ul>
<li>Web site authors cannot perfectly conceal how their JavaScript operates because the raw source code must be sent to the client. The code can be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Obfuscated_code" title="Obfuscated code">obfuscated</a>, but obfuscation can be reverse-engineered.</li>
<li>JavaScript form validation only provides convenience for users, not security. If a site verifies that the user agreed to its terms of service, or filters invalid characters out of fields that should only contain numbers, it must do so on the server, not only the client.</li>
<li>Scripts can be selectively disabled, so JavaScript can't be relied on to prevent operations such as right-clicking on an image to save it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></li>
<li>It is extremely bad practice to embed sensitive information such as passwords in JavaScript because it can be extracted by an attacker.</li>
</ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Browser_and_plugin_coding_errors">Browser and plugin coding errors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=JavaScript&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Browser and plugin coding errors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>JavaScript provides an interface to a wide range of browser capabilities, some of which may have flaws such as <a href="/wiki/Buffer_overflow" title="Buffer overflow">buffer overflows</a>. These flaws can allow attackers to write scripts that would run any code they wish on the user's system. This code is not by any means limited to another JavaScript application. For example, a buffer overrun exploit can allow an attacker to gain access to the operating system's API with superuser privileges.</p>
<p>These flaws have affected major browsers including Firefox,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> Internet Explorer,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> and Safari.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<p>Plugins, such as video players, <a href="/wiki/Adobe_Flash#Flash_client_security" title="Adobe Flash">Adobe Flash</a>, and the wide range of <a href="/wiki/ActiveX" title="ActiveX">ActiveX</a> controls enabled by default in Microsoft Internet Explorer, may also have flaws exploitable via JavaScript (such flaws have been exploited in the past).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<p>In Windows Vista, Microsoft has attempted to contain the risks of bugs such as buffer overflows by running the Internet Explorer process with limited privileges.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Google_Chrome" title="Google Chrome">Google Chrome</a> similarly confines its page renderers to their own "sandbox".</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sandbox_implementation_errors">Sandbox implementation errors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=JavaScript&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Sandbox implementation errors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Web browsers are capable of running JavaScript outside the sandbox, with the privileges necessary to, for example, create or delete files. Of course, such privileges aren't meant to be granted to code from the Web.</p>
<p>Incorrectly granting privileges to JavaScript from the Web has played a role in vulnerabilities in both Internet Explorer<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> and Firefox.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> In Windows XP Service Pack 2, Microsoft demoted JScript's privileges in Internet Explorer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Windows" title="Microsoft Windows">Microsoft Windows</a> allows JavaScript source files on a computer's hard drive to be launched as general-purpose, non-sandboxed programs (see: <a href="/wiki/Windows_Script_Host" title="Windows Script Host">Windows Script Host</a>). This makes JavaScript (like <a href="/wiki/VBScript" title="VBScript">VBScript</a>) a theoretically viable vector for a <a href="/wiki/Trojan_horse_(computing)" title="Trojan horse (computing)">Trojan horse</a>, although JavaScript Trojan horses are uncommon in practice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Uses_outside_Web_pages">Uses outside Web pages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=JavaScript&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Uses outside Web pages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In addition to Web browsers and servers, JavaScript interpreters are embedded in a number of tools. Each of these applications provides its own <a href="/wiki/Object_model" title="Object model">object model</a> that provides access to the host environment. The core JavaScript language remains mostly the same in each application.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Embedded_scripting_language">Embedded scripting language</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=JavaScript&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Embedded scripting language">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li>Google's <a href="/wiki/Google_Chrome" title="Google Chrome">Chrome</a> extensions, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Opera_(Web_browser)" title="Opera (Web browser)">Opera</a>'s extensions, Apple's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Safari_(Web_browser)" title="Safari (Web browser)">Safari 5</a> extensions, Apple's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dashboard_(Mac_OS)" title="Dashboard (Mac OS)">Dashboard Widgets</a>, Microsoft's <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Gadgets" title="Microsoft Gadgets">Gadgets</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yahoo!_Widgets" title="Yahoo! Widgets">Yahoo! Widgets</a>, <a href="/wiki/Google_Desktop#Gadgets_and_plug-ins" title="Google Desktop">Google Desktop Gadgets</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Serence" title="Serence">Serence</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Klipfolio" title="Klipfolio">Klipfolio</a> are implemented using JavaScript.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/MongoDB" title="MongoDB">MongoDB</a> database accepts queries written in JavaScript. <a href="/wiki/MongoDB" title="MongoDB">MongoDB</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NodeJS" title="NodeJS">NodeJS</a> are the core components of <a href="/wiki/MEAN_(software_bundle)" title="MEAN (software bundle)">MEAN</a>: a <a href="/wiki/Solution_stack" title="Solution stack">solution stack</a> for creating Web applications using just JavaScript.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Clusterpoint" title="Clusterpoint">Clusterpoint</a> database accept queries written in JS/SQL, which is a combination of <a href="/wiki/SQL" title="SQL">SQL</a> and JavaScript. <a href="/wiki/Clusterpoint" title="Clusterpoint">Clusterpoint</a> has built-in computing engine that allows execution of JavaScript code right inside the <a href="/wiki/Distributed_database" title="Distributed database">distributed database</a>.</li>
<li>Adobe's <a href="/wiki/Adobe_Acrobat#Security" title="Adobe Acrobat">Acrobat and Adobe Reader</a> support JavaScript in <a href="/wiki/Portable_Document_Format" title="Portable Document Format">PDF</a> files.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup></li>
<li>Tools in the <a href="/wiki/Adobe_Creative_Suite" title="Adobe Creative Suite">Adobe Creative Suite</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Adobe_Photoshop" title="Adobe Photoshop">Photoshop</a>, <a href="/wiki/Adobe_Illustrator" title="Adobe Illustrator">Illustrator</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dreamweaver" title="Dreamweaver">Dreamweaver</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/InDesign" title="InDesign">InDesign</a>, allow scripting through JavaScript.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/OpenOffice.org" title="OpenOffice.org">OpenOffice.org</a>, an office application suite, as well as its popular fork <a href="/wiki/LibreOffice" title="LibreOffice">LibreOffice</a>, allows JavaScript to be used as a scripting language.</li>
<li>The visual programming language <a href="/wiki/Max_(software)" title="Max (software)">Max</a>, released by Cycling '74, offers a JavaScript model of its environment for use by developers. It allows users to reduce visual clutter by using a object for a task rather than many.</li>
<li>Apple's Logic Pro X digital audio workstation (DAW) software can create custom MIDI effects plugins using JavaScript.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></li>
<li>ECMAScript was included in the <a href="/wiki/VRML" title="VRML">VRML</a>97 standard for scripting nodes of VRML scene description files.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Unity_(game_engine)" title="Unity (game engine)">Unity</a> game engine supports a modified version of JavaScript for scripting via Mono.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/DX_Studio" title="DX Studio">DX Studio</a> (3D engine) uses the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SpiderMonkey_(JavaScript_engine)" title="SpiderMonkey (JavaScript engine)">SpiderMonkey</a> implementation of JavaScript for game and simulation logic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Maxwell_Render" title="Maxwell Render">Maxwell Render</a> (<a href="/wiki/Rendering_(computer_graphics)" title="Rendering (computer graphics)">rendering</a> software) provides an ECMA standard based scripting engine for tasks automation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Google_Apps_Script" title="Google Apps Script">Google Apps Script</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Google_Spreadsheets" title="Google Spreadsheets">Google Spreadsheets</a> and <a href="/wiki/Google_Sites" title="Google Sites">Google Sites</a> allows users to create custom formulas, automate repetitive tasks and also interact with other Google products such as Gmail.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup></li>
<li>Many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Internet_Relay_Chat_clients" title="Internet Relay Chat clients">IRC clients</a>, like <a href="/wiki/ChatZilla" title="ChatZilla">ChatZilla</a> or <a href="/wiki/XChat" title="XChat">XChat</a>, use JavaScript for their scripting abilities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/RPG_Maker" title="RPG Maker">RPG Maker</a> MV uses Javascript as its scripting language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Scripting_engine">Scripting engine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=JavaScript&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Scripting engine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li>Microsoft's <a href="/wiki/Active_Scripting" title="Active Scripting">Active Scripting</a> technology supports <a href="/wiki/JScript" title="JScript">JScript</a> as a scripting language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VersionInformation_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-VersionInformation-80">[80]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Java_(programming_language)" title="Java (programming language)">Java</a> introduced the <tt>javax.script</tt> package in version 6 that includes a JavaScript implementation based on <a href="/wiki/Rhino_(JavaScript_engine)" title="Rhino (JavaScript engine)">Mozilla Rhino</a>. Thus, Java applications can host scripts that access the application's variables and objects, much like Web browsers host scripts that access a webpage's <a href="/wiki/Document_Object_Model" title="Document Object Model">Document Object Model</a> (DOM).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlanagan2006214_et_seq_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlanagan2006214_et_seq-82">[82]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qt_(toolkit)" title="Qt (toolkit)">Qt</a> C++ toolkit includes a <tt>QtScript</tt> module to interpret JavaScript, analogous to Java's <tt>javax.script</tt> package.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/OS_X_Yosemite" title="OS X Yosemite">OS X Yosemite</a> introduced JavaScript for Automation (JXA), which is built upon <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/JavaScriptCore" title="JavaScriptCore">JavaScriptCore</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Open_Scripting_Architecture" title="Open Scripting Architecture">Open Scripting Architecture</a>. It features an <a href="/wiki/Objective-C" title="Objective-C">Objective-C</a> bridge that enables entire <a href="/wiki/Cocoa_(API)" title="Cocoa (API)">Cocoa</a> applications to be programmed in JavaScript.</li>
<li>Late Night Software's <a href="/wiki/JavaScript_OSA" title="JavaScript OSA">JavaScript OSA</a> (also known as JavaScript for OSA, or JSOSA) is a freeware alternative to <a href="/wiki/AppleScript" title="AppleScript">AppleScript</a> for OS X. It is based on the Mozilla JavaScript 1.5 implementation, with the addition of a <tt>MacOS</tt> object for interaction with the operating system and third-party applications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Application_platform">Application platform</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=JavaScript&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Application platform">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/ActionScript" title="ActionScript">ActionScript</a>, the programming language used in <a href="/wiki/Adobe_Flash" title="Adobe Flash">Adobe Flash</a>, is another implementation of the ECMAScript standard.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adobe_Integrated_Runtime" title="Adobe Integrated Runtime">Adobe Integrated Runtime</a> is a JavaScript runtime that allows developers to create desktop applications.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Atom_(text_editor)" title="Atom (text editor)">Atom</a>, the open-source text editor developed by Github, was implemented using Javascript, and has a special API on Javascript for packages that are developed for it.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CA,_Inc." title="CA, Inc.">CA, Inc.</a>'s AutoShell cross-application scripting environment is built on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SpiderMonkey_(JavaScript_engine)" title="SpiderMonkey (JavaScript engine)">SpiderMonkey</a> JavaScript engine. It contains <a href="/wiki/Preprocessor" title="Preprocessor">preprocessor</a>-like extensions for command definition, as well as custom classes for various system-related tasks like file I/O, operation system command invocation and redirection, and COM scripting.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/GNOME_Shell" title="GNOME Shell">GNOME Shell</a>, the shell for the <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/GNOME" title="GNOME">GNOME</a> 3</span> desktop environment,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> made JavaScript its default programming language in 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Mozilla" title="Mozilla">Mozilla</a> platform, which underlies <a href="/wiki/Firefox" title="Firefox">Firefox</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mozilla_Thunderbird" title="Mozilla Thunderbird">Thunderbird</a>, and some other Web browsers, uses JavaScript to implement the <a href="/wiki/Graphical_user_interface" title="Graphical user interface">graphical user interface</a> (GUI) of its various products.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Qt_Quick" title="Qt Quick">Qt Quick</a>'s markup language (available since Qt 4.7) uses JavaScript for its application logic. Its declarative syntax is also similar to JavaScript.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/TypeScript" title="TypeScript">TypeScript</a> is a programming language based on JavaScript that adds support for optional <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Type_annotation" title="Type annotation">type annotations</a> and some other language extensions such as classes, interfaces and modules. A TS-script compiles into plain JavaScript and can be executed in any JS host supporting <a href="/wiki/ECMAScript" title="ECMAScript">ECMAScript</a> 3 or higher. The compiler is itself written in TypeScript.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ubuntu_Touch" title="Ubuntu Touch">Ubuntu Touch</a> provides a JavaScript API for its unified usability interface.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/WebOS" title="WebOS">webOS</a> uses the <a href="/wiki/WebKit" title="WebKit">WebKit</a> implementation of JavaScript in its <a href="/wiki/Software_development_kit" title="Software development kit">SDK</a> to allow developers to create stand-alone applications solely in JavaScript.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Windows_Runtime#JavaScript" title="Windows Runtime">WinJS</a> provides a special Windows Library for JavaScript functionality in <a href="/wiki/Windows_8" title="Windows 8">Windows 8</a> that enables the development of <a href="/wiki/Metro_(design_language)" title="Metro (design language)">Modern style</a> (formerly <i>Metro style</i>) applications in <a href="/wiki/HTML5" title="HTML5">HTML5</a> and JavaScript.</li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Development_tools">Development tools</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=JavaScript&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Development tools">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Within JavaScript, access to a <a href="/wiki/Debugger" title="Debugger">debugger</a> becomes invaluable when developing large, non-trivial programs. Because there can be implementation differences between the various browsers (particularly within the DOM), it is useful to have access to a debugger for each of the browsers that a Web application targets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<p>Script debuggers are integrated within <a href="/wiki/Internet_Explorer" title="Internet Explorer">Internet Explorer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Firefox" title="Firefox">Firefox</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Safari_(Web_browser)" title="Safari (Web browser)">Safari</a>, <a href="/wiki/Google_Chrome" title="Google Chrome">Google Chrome</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Opera_(Web_browser)" title="Opera (Web browser)">Opera</a> and <a href="/wiki/Node.js" title="Node.js">Node.js</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup></p>
<p>In addition to the native <a href="/wiki/Internet_Explorer_Developer_Tools" title="Internet Explorer Developer Tools">Internet Explorer Developer Tools</a>, three debuggers are available for Internet Explorer: <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Visual_Studio" title="Microsoft Visual Studio">Microsoft Visual Studio</a> is the richest of the three, closely followed by <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Script_Editor" title="Microsoft Script Editor">Microsoft Script Editor</a> (a component of <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Office" title="Microsoft Office">Microsoft Office</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> and finally the free <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Script_Debugger" title="Microsoft Script Debugger">Microsoft Script Debugger</a> that is far more basic than the other two. The free <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microsoft_Visual_Web_Developer_Express" title="Microsoft Visual Web Developer Express">Microsoft Visual Web Developer Express</a> provides a limited version of the JavaScript debugging functionality in Microsoft Visual Studio. Internet Explorer has included developer tools since version 8.</p>
<p>In comparison to Internet Explorer, Firefox has a more comprehensive set of developer tools, which include a debugger as well. Old versions of Firefox without these tools used a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Firefox_addon" title="Firefox addon">Firefox addon</a> called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Firebug_(Firefox_extension)" title="Firebug (Firefox extension)">Firebug</a>, or the older <a href="/wiki/Venkman" title="Venkman">Venkman</a> debugger. Also, <a href="/wiki/WebKit" title="WebKit">WebKit</a>'s Web Inspector includes a JavaScript debugger,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> which is used in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Safari_(Web_browser)" title="Safari (Web browser)">Safari</a>. A modified version called Blink DevTools is used in <a href="/wiki/Google_Chrome" title="Google Chrome">Google Chrome</a>. <a href="/wiki/Node.js" title="Node.js">Node.js</a> has Node Inspector, an interactive debugger that integrates with the Blink DevTools, available in <a href="/wiki/Google_Chrome" title="Google Chrome">Google Chrome</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Opera_(Web_browser)" title="Opera (Web browser)">Opera</a> includes a set of tools called <a href="/wiki/Opera_Dragonfly" title="Opera Dragonfly">Dragonfly</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup></p>
<p><span id="Online_dev_tools"></span>In addition to the native computer software, there are online JavaScript IDEs, debugging aids that are themselves written in JavaScript and built to run on the Web. An example is the program <a href="/wiki/JSLint" title="JSLint">JSLint</a>, developed by <a href="/wiki/Douglas_Crockford" title="Douglas Crockford">Douglas Crockford</a> who has written extensively on the language. JSLint scans JavaScript code for conformance to a set of standards and guidelines. Many libraries for JavaScript, such as <a href="/wiki/Three.js" title="Three.js">three.js</a>, provide links to demonstration code that can be edited by users. They are also used as a pedagogical tool by institutions such as <a href="/wiki/Khan_Academy" title="Khan Academy">Khan Academy</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> to allow students to experience writing code in an environment where they can see the output of their programs, without needing any setup beyond a Web browser.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Benchmark_tools_for_developers">Benchmark tools for developers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=JavaScript&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Benchmark tools for developers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Since JavaScript is getting more important for web development (frontend overtakes many aspects which were done in backend before), there is also more consideration done about performance. Especially mobile devices could have problems with rendering and processing unoptimized complex logic.</p>
<p>A library for doing benchmarks is <a class="external text" href="https://benchmarkjs.com/" rel="nofollow">benchmark.js</a>. A benchmarking library that supports high-resolution timers and returns statistically significant results<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="Need citation on statistically significant results (November 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>.</p>
<p>Another tool is <a class="external text" href="http://jsben.ch" rel="nofollow">jsben.ch</a>. An online JavaScript benchmarking tool, where code snippets can be tested against each other.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Version_history">Version history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=JavaScript&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Version history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/ECMAScript#Versions" title="ECMAScript">ECMAScript § Versions</a>, and <a href="/wiki/ECMAScript#Version_correspondence" title="ECMAScript">ECMAScript § Version correspondence</a></div>
<p>JavaScript was initially developed in 1996 for use in the <a href="/wiki/Netscape_Navigator" title="Netscape Navigator">Netscape Navigator</a> Web browser. In the same year Microsoft released an implementation for Internet Explorer. This implementation was called <a href="/wiki/JScript" title="JScript">JScript</a> due to trademark issues. In 1997 the first standardized version of the language was released under the name <a href="/wiki/ECMAScript" title="ECMAScript">ECMAScript</a> in the first edition of the ECMA-252 standard. The explicit versioning and opt-in of language features was Mozilla-specific and has been removed. Firefox 4 was the last version which referred to a JavaScript version (1.8.5). With new editions of the ECMA-262 standard, JavaScript language features are now often mentioned with their initial definition in the ECMA-262 editions.</p>
<p>The following table is based on information from multiple sources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Related_languages_and_features">Related languages and features</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=JavaScript&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Related languages and features">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><a href="/wiki/JSON" title="JSON">JSON</a>, or JavaScript Object Notation, is a general-purpose data interchange format that is defined as a subset of JavaScript's object literal syntax. Like much of JavaScript (regexps and anonymous functions as 1st class elements, closures, flexible classes, 'use strict'), <a href="/wiki/JSON" title="JSON">JSON</a>, except for replacing <a href="/wiki/Perl" title="Perl">Perl</a>'s key-value operator '=&gt;' by an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/RFC_822" title="RFC 822">RFC 822</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> inspired ':', is syntactically pure Perl.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/JQuery" title="JQuery">jQuery</a> is a popular JavaScript library designed to simplify <a href="/wiki/Document_Object_Model" title="Document Object Model">DOM</a>-oriented client-side HTML scripting along with offering cross-browser compatibility because various browsers respond differently to certain vanilla JavaScript code.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Underscore.js" title="Underscore.js">Underscore.js</a> is a utility JavaScript library for data manipulation that is used in both client-side and server-side network applications.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/AngularJS" title="AngularJS">AngularJS</a> is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Web_application_framework" title="Web application framework">web application framework</a> to use for developing <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Single-page_applications&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Single-page applications (page does not exist)">single-page applications</a> and also cross-platform mobile apps.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/React_(JavaScript_library)" title="React (JavaScript library)">React (JavaScript library)</a> is an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Open-source" title="Open-source">open-source</a> JavaScript library providing a views that is rendered using components specified as custom HTML tags.</p>
<p>Mozilla browsers currently support <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LiveConnect" title="LiveConnect">LiveConnect</a>, a feature that allows JavaScript and Java to intercommunicate on the Web. However, Mozilla-specific support for LiveConnect is scheduled to be phased out in the future in favor of passing on the LiveConnect handling via <a href="/wiki/NPAPI" title="NPAPI">NPAPI</a> to the Java 1.6+ plug-in (not yet supported on the Mac as of March 2010<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=JavaScript&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> Most browser inspection tools, such as <a href="/wiki/Firebug_(software)" title="Firebug (software)">Firebug</a> in Firefox, include JavaScript interpreters that can act on the visible page's DOM.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Asm.js" title="Asm.js">asm.js</a> is a subset of JavaScript that can be run in any JavaScript engine or run faster in an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ahead-of-time" title="Ahead-of-time">ahead-of-time</a> (AOT) compiling engine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup></p>
<p><a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/JSFuck" title="JSFuck">JSFuck</a> is an <a href="/wiki/Esoteric_programming_language" title="Esoteric programming language">esoteric programming language</a>. Programs are written using only six different characters, but are still valid JavaScript code.</p>
<p>p5.js<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> is an object oriented JavaScript library designed for artists and designers. It is based on the ideas of the <a href="/wiki/Processing_(programming_language)" title="Processing (programming language)">Processing</a> project but is for the web.</p>
<p><a class="external text" href="http://jsben.ch" rel="nofollow">jsben.ch</a> is an online JavaScript benchmarking tool, where different code snippets can be tested against each other.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Use_as_an_intermediate_language">Use as an intermediate language</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=JavaScript&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Use as an intermediate language">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As JavaScript is the most widely supported client-side language that can run within a Web browser, it has become an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intermediate_language" title="Intermediate language">intermediate language</a> for other languages to target. This has included both newly created languages and ports of existing languages. Some of these include:</p>
<ul>
<li>OberonScript, a full implementation of the <a href="/wiki/Oberon_(programming_language)" title="Oberon (programming language)">Oberon</a> programming language that compiles to high-level JavaScript.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Objective-J" title="Objective-J">Objective-J</a>, a superset of JavaScript that compiles to standard JavaScript. It adds traditional inheritance and <a href="/wiki/Smalltalk" title="Smalltalk">Smalltalk</a>/<a href="/wiki/Objective-C" title="Objective-C">Objective-C</a> style dynamic dispatch and optional pseudo-static typing to JavaScript.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Processing.js" title="Processing.js">Processing.js</a>, a JavaScript port of the Processing programming language designed to write visualizations, images, and interactive content. It allows Web browsers to display animations, visual applications, games and other graphical rich content without the need for a Java applet or Flash plugin.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/CoffeeScript" title="CoffeeScript">CoffeeScript</a>, an alternate syntax for JavaScript intended to be more concise and readable. It adds features like array comprehensions (also available in JavaScript since version 1.7)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> and pattern matching. Like Objective-J, it compiles to JavaScript. Ruby and Python have been cited as influential on CoffeeScript syntax.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Google_Web_Toolkit" title="Google Web Toolkit">Google Web Toolkit</a> translates a subset of Java to JavaScript.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Scala_(programming_language)" title="Scala (programming language)">Scala</a>, an object-oriented and functional programming language, has a Scala-to-JavaScript compiler.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pyjamas_(software)" title="Pyjamas (software)">Pyjamas</a>, a port of <a href="/wiki/Google_Web_Toolkit" title="Google Web Toolkit">Google Web Toolkit</a> to <a href="/wiki/Python_(programming_language)" title="Python (programming language)">Python</a> translates a subset of Python to JavaScript.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Dart_(programming_language)" title="Dart (programming language)">Dart</a>, an open-source programming language developed by Google, can be compiled to JavaScript.</li>
<li>Whalesong,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> a <a href="/wiki/Racket_(programming_language)" title="Racket (programming language)">Racket</a>-to-JavaScript compiler.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Emscripten" title="Emscripten">Emscripten</a>, a <a href="/wiki/LLVM" title="LLVM">LLVM</a>-backend for porting native libraries to JavaScript.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Fantom_(programming_language)" title="Fantom (programming language)">Fantom</a> a programming language that runs on JVM, .NET and JavaScript.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/TypeScript" title="TypeScript">TypeScript</a>, a free and open-source programming language developed by Microsoft. It is a superset of JavaScript, and essentially adds optional static typing and class-based object-oriented programming to the language.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Haxe" title="Haxe">Haxe</a>, an open-source high-level multiplatform programming language and compiler that can produce applications and source code for many different platforms including JavaScript.</li>
<li>ClojureScript,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> a compiler for <a href="/wiki/Clojure" title="Clojure">Clojure</a> that targets JavaScript. It is designed to emit JavaScript code that is compatible with the advanced compilation mode of the Google Closure optimizing compiler.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Kotlin_(programming_language)" title="Kotlin (programming language)">Kotlin</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Type_system#Static_type-checking" title="Type system">statically-typed</a> language that also compiles to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Java_byte_code" title="Java byte code">Java byte code</a>.</li>
<li><a class="external text" href="https://bertfreudenberg.github.io/SqueakJS/" rel="nofollow">SqueakJS</a>, a virtual machine and DOM environment for the open-source <a href="/wiki/Squeak" title="Squeak">Squeak</a> implementation of the <a href="/wiki/Smalltalk" title="Smalltalk">Smalltalk</a> programming language.</li>
</ul>
<p>As JavaScript has unusual limitations – such as no explicit integer type, only double-precision binary floating point – languages that compile to JavaScript and do not take care to use the integer-converting shift and bitwise logical operators may have slightly different behavior than in other environments.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="JavaScript_and_Java">JavaScript and Java</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=JavaScript&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: JavaScript and Java">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A common misconception is that JavaScript is similar or closely related to <a href="/wiki/Java_(programming_language)" title="Java (programming language)">Java</a>. It is true that both have a C-like syntax (the C language being their most immediate common ancestor language). They also are both typically <a href="/wiki/Sandbox_(computer_security)" title="Sandbox (computer security)">sandboxed</a> (when used inside a browser), and JavaScript was designed with Java's syntax and standard library in mind. In particular, all Java keywords were reserved in original JavaScript, JavaScript's standard library follows Java's naming conventions, and JavaScript's Math and Date objects are based on classes from Java 1.0,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-popularity_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-popularity-109">[109]</a></sup> but the similarities end there.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Java_(programming_language)" title="Java (programming language)">Java</a> and JavaScript both first appeared on 23 May 1995, but Java was developed by James Gosling of Sun Microsystems, and JavaScript by Brendan Eich of NetScape Communications.</p>
<p>The differences between the two languages are more prominent than their similarities. Java has <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Static_typing" title="Static typing">static typing</a>, while JavaScript's typing is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dynamic_typing" title="Dynamic typing">dynamic</a>. Java is loaded from compiled bytecode, while JavaScript is loaded as human-readable source code. Java's objects are <a href="/wiki/Class-based_programming" title="Class-based programming">class-based</a>, while JavaScript's are <a href="/wiki/Prototype-based_programming" title="Prototype-based programming">prototype-based</a>. Finally, Java did not support <a href="/wiki/Functional_programming" title="Functional programming">functional programming</a> until Java 8, while JavaScript has done so from the beginning, being influenced by <a href="/wiki/Scheme_(programming_language)" title="Scheme (programming language)">Scheme</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=JavaScript&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
